        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b08237"><meta property="og:title" content="Fe7S8 Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/acsami.8b08237.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Despite high sodium storage capacity and better reversibility, metal sulfides suffer from relatively low conductivity and severe volume change as anode materials of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Introducing a conductive carbon matrix is an efficient method to enhance their sodium storage performance. Herein, we present iron sulfide (Fe7S8) nanoparticles anchored on nitrogen-doped graphene nanosheets fabricated through a combined strategy of solvothermal and postheating process. The as-prepared composite exhibits appealing cycling stability (a high discharge capacity of 393.1 mA h g–1 over 500 cycles at a current density of 400 mA g–1 and outstanding high-rate performance of 543 mA h g–1 even at 10 A g–1). Considering the excellent sodium storage performance, this composite is quite hopeful to become a potential candidate as anode materials for future SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/acsami.8b08237.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8fb75e5db325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8fb75e5db325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '5c406444-1ee1-4d61-8edc-86f091dfd4ff'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da8fb75e5db325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8fb75e5db325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.8b08237" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90da8fb75e5db325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fe&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt;S&lt;sub&gt;8&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles Anchored on Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Nanosheets as Anode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08237", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Jianhua++Yang" data-id="article_author_search">Jianhua Yang</a></span></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Zhaoyin Wen</span><strong>*</strong><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Zhaoyin Wen</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">CAS Key Laboratory of Materials for Energy Conversion, Shanghai Institute of Ceramics, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Shanghai 200050, P. China</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>E-mail: <a href="mailto:zywen@mail.sic.ac.cn">zywen@mail.sic.ac.cn</a>. Tel: +86-21-52411704. Fax: +86-21-52413903.</span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Zhaoyin++Wen" data-id="article_author_search">Zhaoyin Wen</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-1698-7420" title="Orcid issue&nbsp;</span><time datetime="2018-09-05" class="article__history__time">5 September 2018</time></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden" class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D29476%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DQiming%2BHe%252C%2BKun%2BRui%252C%2BJianhua%2BYang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D35%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b08237%26title%3DFe7S8%2BNanoparticles%2BAnchored%2Bon%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BGraphene%2BNanosheets%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D29485%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2018" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/medium/am-2018-08237t_0003.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Despite high sodium storage capacity and better reversibility, metal sulfides suffer from relatively low conductivity and severe volume change as anode materials of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Introducing a conductive carbon matrix is an efficient method to enhance their sodium storage performance. Herein, we present iron sulfide (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>) nanoparticles anchored on nitrogen-doped graphene nanosheets fabricated through a combined strategy of solvothermal and postheating process. The as-prepared composite exhibits appealing cycling stability (a high discharge capacity of 393.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 500 cycles at a current density of 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and outstanding high-rate performance of 543 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Considering the excellent sodium storage performance, this composite is quite hopeful to become a potential candidate as anode materials for future SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D29476%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DQiming%2BHe%252C%2BKun%2BRui%252C%2BJianhua%2BYang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D35%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b08237%26title%3DFe7S8%2BNanoparticles%2BAnchored%2Bon%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BGraphene%2BNanosheets%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D29485%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290687&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical conductivity" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20conductivity" class="article__tags__link">Electrical conductivity</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanoparticles" class="article__tags__link">Nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291840&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sulfides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sulfides" class="article__tags__link">Sulfides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nitrogen-doped+Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nitrogen-doped%20graphene">nitrogen-doped graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20materials">anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Iron+Sulfides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_iron%20sulfides">iron sulfides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Conversion+Reaction&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_conversion%20reaction">conversion reaction</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Benefitting from elemental abundance and cost-efficiency, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as one of the most attractive alternatives to lithium-ion batteries. However, it is a critical challenge to achieve high-performance SIBs and design viable anode materials which can deliver stable cyclability and high capacity. In recent years, transition metal sulfides (TMSs), such as MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> SnS, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> SnS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> CoS, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> and CoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> have intrigued many researchers’ interests, due to their large sodium storage capacities and robust structures as anode materials for SIBs. Moreover, studies have shown that the M–S ionic bonds are comparably weaker than M–O bonds, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> which is favorable for the electrochemical reversibility of TMSs compared with transition metal oxides. In addition, with advantages like environmental friendliness, cost-effectiveness, and earth abundance, iron sulfides, involving FeS <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> and FeS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> hold eminent position among anode materials of SIBs. Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, known as pyrrhotite, possesses both the mixed-valence state and intrinsic metallic character, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> which are beneficial for anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> Unfortunately, their potential applications are hindered by huge volume variation, low conductivity, and sluggish kinetics during cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the above concern, considerable efforts have been dedicated to improving the electrochemical performances of iron sulfides through lowering the particle size to nanoscale and introducing conductive carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Nanoparticles have much higher specific area than that of bulk materials, which may contribute a lot more active sites for electrochemical reactions and provide more contact between the electrode and the electrolyte, benefiting the fast sodium-ion storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Furthermore, anchoring the iron sulfide nanoparticles onto carbonaceous materials can efficiently buffer the volume expansion and enhance electronic conductivity. Two-dimensional (2D) graphene nanosheet has been universally exploited as such matrix because of its excellent electronic conductivity, considerable surface area, and superb mechanical elasticity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Moreover, it is notable that the heteroatom doping in graphene, such as nitrogen, has been reported to remarkably enhance the electronic conductivity and the surface hydrophilicity of graphene, benefiting the electron/ion transportation and the electrode–electrolyte contact. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Considering the above advantages, the nanocomposite of iron sulfide nanoparticles and nitrogen-doped graphene nanosheets (FS–NGNs) hold good potential to become a suitable anode material of high-performance SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we have successfully anchored the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles onto nitrogen-doped graphene nanosheets through a one-pot solvothermal procedure combined with postheating treatment. The as-prepared composite (denoted as FS–NGNs) showed superior cycling performance. After 500 cycles at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the FS–NGNs retained a high capacity of 393.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, achieving a Coulombic efficiency of 99%. Even at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the composite can still deliver a high capacity of 543 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The high sodium storage capacity and excellent rate performance can meet the request for future application of SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphene oxide (GO) was synthesized according to a modified Hummers’ method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> To prepare the FS–NGNs, first, certain amount of GO was dispersed into 46 mL of deionized water by ultrasonication and vigorous stirring for 1 h, respectively. Then 3 mmol of FeSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O and 6 mmol of thioacetamide (TAA) were dissolved into the above mixture. Finally, 23 mL of diethylenetriamine (DETA) was added dropwise. The mixture was then transferred to a Teflon-lined autoclave and heated at 140 °C for 24 h. The product was subsequently filtered and rinsed with deionized water and pure ethanol three times and dried in a vacuum at 60 °C overnight. The FS–NGNs were obtained after a postheating treatment under N<sub>2</sub> flow at 700 °C for 3 h. The samples of FS–NGNs with different weight ratios of graphene were fabricated for comparison and denoted as FS–NGNs-60, FS–NGNs-80, and FS–NGNs-100 according to the initial GO masses in the mixture during the synthesis procedure. For example, FS–NGNs-80 is in accordance with 80 mg GO in the starting mixture. The bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles were also synthesized without adding GO into the precursor mixture. Other conditions were controlled as the same as those of the FS–NGNs. The reduced graphene oxide (RGO) was synthesized by the hydrothermal process without adding Fe resource, TAA, and DETA.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystalline phases of the as-prepared materials were analyzed by an X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku, Ultima IV) with Cu Kα radiation. Raman spectroscopy was performed using a HORIBA LabRAM HR Evolution equipped with an Ar-ion laser at an excitation wavelength of 532 nm. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed using an ESCALAB 250Xi equipped with an Al Kα X-ray source. The thermogravimetric (TG) analysis (TGA) data was collected by a thermal analysis equipment (NETZSCH STA 449C) with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> from room 50 to 900 °C in air. The morphology and composition of the as-prepared samples were analyzed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, Magellan 400) equipped with an energy-dispersive spectrometer (EDS) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEM-2100F).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All the electrochemical tests were conducted using half cells (CR2025-type). The electrodes were fabricated through uniform coating of the aqueous slurry consisting of the active material, Super P (conductive carbon), polymerized styrene butadiene rubber, as well as the binder carboxymethyl cellulose with a mass ratio of 14:4:1:1 onto copper foil. After being dried at 60 °C for 12 h under vacuum conditions, the CR2025-type half cells were assembled in a Mikrouna glovebox in an argon atmosphere, where both H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> contents were below 0.1 ppm. The electrolyte consists of NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1 M) in propylene carbonate with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate as the additive. The glass fiber (φ 18 mm) and metallic sodium (Sigma-Aldrich) were used as a separator and reference electrode, respectively. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles were collected by a Chenhua CHI600E electrochemical analyzer. The galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements were conducted by utilizing a LAND charge–discharge instrument (Wuhan, China). The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis was carried out through a Metrohm Autolab instrument between 0.01 Hz and 1000 kHz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.1.  Phase and Morphology Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The FS–NGNs were synthesized through a simple one-pot solvothermal reaction combined with a postheating process, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. Firstly, GO was uniformly distributed in deionized water. After adding an Fe resource, the negative charges from the functional group on the surface of GO can absorb Fe ions. Then, TAA was introduced as the sulfur resource. Finally, DETA was added and also absorbed by GO due to the positive charges of the amino group. During the solvothermal reaction, TAA decomposed and reacted with Fe ions. DETA was exploited as a chelating agent to coordinate with Fe ions to avoid oversize growth. Meanwhile, its amino group can reduce GO and dope N atoms into the graphene matrix. After the solvothermal process, the precursor was further annealed for Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> crystallization, obtaining the final FS–NGNs.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/medium/am-2018-08237t_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Typical synthesis procedure of FS–NGNs<sup>a</sup></div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label"><sup>a</sup></span>The red, yellow, gray, and blue spheres represent Fe ions, S, C, and N atoms, respectively. The brown network represents the GO nanosheet.</p><p></p></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis was conducted to characterize the phase and the structure of the FS–NGNs. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the main peaks in the XRD pattern is well indexed to Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (PDF #25-0411). Meanwhile, there are some unindexed minor peaks, which may be attributed to some impurities, such as oxidized surface species or other form of iron sulfides. There are no visible diffraction peaks of graphene at 2θ = 20–30°, indicating that Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles effectively coat the graphene surface to restrain the restacking of graphene nanosheets during the synthesis procedure. Furthermore, the phase of the as-prepared Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles without graphene was also characterized by XRD, which indicates the almost pure phase of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Raman spectroscopy characterization was also carried out to further identify the composition of the FS–NGN composite. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, the Raman shifts positioned at 1353 and 1593 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to the D-band and G-band of graphene, respectively. Compared to GO, the ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> in NGNs is slightly lower for FS–NGNs (1.11 for GO vs 1.03 for FS–NGNs), suggesting a higher graphitization extent of graphene originating from the sp<sup>2</sup> restoration during the heating process, which is crucial for the enhancement of electronic conductivity. The chemical states of the elements in FS–NGNs were investigated by high-resolution X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). As can be seen in the Fe 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), the peaks at 710.4 and 715 eV (2p<sub>3/2</sub> with shake-up satellite) indicate the existence of Fe<sup>2+</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> whereas the peaks at 723.6 and 731.1 eV (2p<sub>1/2</sub> with shake-up satellite) can be attributed to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> In the S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d), the peaks at 161.65, 162.75, and 163.8 eV can be indexed to metal–sulfur bonding. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 27 to 30">(27−30)</a> Some SO<i><sub>x</sub></i> species are also detected (168.6 eV), probably due to adsorbed oxygen on the active surface of FS–NGNs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The N 1s spectrum reveals pyridinic (398.9 eV) and pyrrolic (400.7 eV) nitrogen in the FS–NGNs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e), confirming the successful doping of N. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> The C 1s spectrum further substantiates the nitrogen doping. The peak at 284.9 eV is assigned to C–C bonding, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> whereas the peak at 285.6 eV is attributed to C–N bonding (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/medium/am-2018-08237t_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0006.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD pattern of the as-synthesized FS–NGNs. (b) Raman spectra of the FS–NGNs and GO. High-resolution XPS spectra of the FS–NGNs: (c) Fe 2p, (d) S 2p, (e) N 1s, and (f) C 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It has been comprehensively studied previously that the morphology of graphene-based materials applied in SIBs also has an important influence on the sodium storage performance. Thus, the morphology and specific microstructural characters of the FS–NGN hybrid anode were further revealed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a exhibits a typical scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of the as-prepared FS–NGN composites, where 2D and broad-piece sheet-like morphologies can be observed with a rough surface and uniform distribution of nanoparticles, indicating the in situ formation of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> on graphene nanosheets, which is further confirmed by the backscattered electronic (BSE) image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). These nanoparticles are around 100–200 nm, which are greatly smaller than bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The distribution of the sulfide nanoparticles is further revealed by EDS elemental mapping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). As can be seen, the high signal intensity positions of Fe and S elements exhibit good conformity and are in good accordance with the positions of the nanoparticles in the SEM image, whereas the spectrum of C illustrates the profile of the graphene matrix. Furthermore, it is critical to mention that a large amount of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles are confined within the graphene layers, displaying the efficiency of self-assembly between the functionalized GO and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b shows a characteristic TEM image of a small piece of the as-prepared FS–NGN composites, further confirming sheet-like graphene without severe restacking and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> uniform distribution without agglomeration. Moreover, the high crystallization character of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> in the FS–NGN composites is also confirmed by the HRTEM image shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c, where series of parallel fringes with <i>d</i>-spacings of 0.298 and 0.265 nm are observed, indicating the (200) and (203) planes of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (hexagonal, <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>), respectively. The pattern of selected area electronic diffraction (SAED, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) displays (200) and (203) planes of nanocrystalline Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, which is in good accordance with the XRD pattern and the HRTEM image.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/medium/am-2018-08237t_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) SEM, (b) TEM, (c) HRTEM, and (d) SAED image of the as-prepared FS–NGNs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Sodium Storage Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical reaction of the as-synthesized FS–NGNs was evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a exhibits the CV profiles of FS–NGNs obtained during the beginning five scans. The first cathodic scan of the FS–NGNs shows a sharp large peak at 0.82 V, indicating the conversion of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> into Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> and a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed due to the decomposition of the electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The cathodic peak at 0.9 V during the following four cycles is also due to the conversion of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> into Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The shifting of 0.08 V is due to the SEI formation and some irreversible metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S during the initial cycle. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> The anodic peaks observed at 1.41 V (sharp) and 1.75 V (broad) are ascribed to the formation of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, which has been reported for various iron sulfides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 11, 39 and 40">(11,39,40)</a> Moreover, the minor peak at 0.09 V is assigned to Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation from nanopores of FS–NGNs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/medium/am-2018-08237t_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) CV curves of the FS–NGNs for the beginning five scans at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Charge–discharge curves of FS–NGNs for the beginning five cycles at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) The cycling performance of FS–NGNs, RGO, and bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particle at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) The rate performance of FS–NGNs at various current densities from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Electrochemical impedance spectra of FS–NGNs and the bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> before and after cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The initial five charge–discharge curves of FS–NGNs at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) are in good accordance with the CV curves. A noticeable plateau at about 0.8 V in the first discharge profile is attributed to the formation of Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The profiles of latter four cycles maintain good conformity. Compared to the FS–NGNs, the charge–discharge profiles of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) exhibit obvious polarization and capacity decay during the initial five cycles. The cycling performances of FS–NGNs, pure graphene, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. Though obtaining high initial capacity, the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles exhibit fast degradation. On the other hand, it can be seen that although the cycling performance of RGO is very steady, its capacity is rather low, suggesting that combining the sulfide nanoparticles is necessary. As for the FS–NGNs, compared with the FS–NGNs-80 sample, FS–NGNs-60 and FS–NGNs-100 exhibit inferior cycling performances. The insufficient graphene in FS–NGNs-60 may lead to overloaded particles on graphene nanosheets or even detachment between them, which will cause uneven distribution of the particles and hider the cycling performance. On the other hand, too much graphene may bring about reduced mass load, which will lower the capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel may also be blocked by overwhelmed graphene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Thus, it can be inferred that 80 mg is in the optimized range of the amount of GO in the as-mentioned precursor. The weight ratio of the N-doped graphene in FS–NGNs-80 was evaluated through thermogravimetric analysis (TGA). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, since Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is fully transformed into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> after 800 °C in air, the weight ratio of graphene in FS–NGNs-80 is estimated to be 25.26 wt %. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 13, 45 and 46">(13,45,46)</a> The initial discharge and charge capacities of FS–NGNs-80 are 758.7 and 647.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Thus, the initial Coulombic efficiency is as high as 85.39%, which can be ascribed to facile and effective ion/electron transport in this electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> The FS–NGNs-80 preserved reversible capacity of ∼630.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup> during the initial 15 cycles. Then, the discharge capacity gradually increases to 827.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for the remaining 125 cycles. The FS–NGNs-80 exhibits satisfying Coulombic efficiencies of more than 97% since the fourth cycle forward. After 500 cycles, the discharge capacity of FS–NGNs-80 is 393.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The primary reason for the increased capacity is the formation of polymeric gel-like film on the active material generated from electrolyte decomposition, which has been noticed in some previous studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 49 to 51">(49−51)</a> In spite of the relatively low original surface area, after the initial sodium insertion, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is transformed into metal nanoparticles in the Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix. Accordingly, the surface area is significantly enlarged. Furthermore, based on the conversion reaction for Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, new surfaces of metallic nanoparticles are exposed during each discharge. Consequently, the decomposition of the electrolyte results in SEI film formation, leading to a steady capacity increase. However, the ongoing electrolyte decomposition and unstable SEI may result in active component surrounded by SEI to form “isolated islands,” and block ion/electron transport, causing the loss of capacity. Moreover, the intermediate sodium polysulfide may react with the carbonate ester electrolyte before Na<sub>2</sub>S formation, which will also lead to capacity loss. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> The above discussion might explain the capacity degradation after the 150th cycle. The charge–discharge profiles of the 1st, 100th, 200th, 300th, 400th, and 500th cycles for the FS–NGNs-80 are also displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. As can be seen, the initial discharge and charge plateaus have gradually disappeared and transformed into slopes. This may be due to the structure modulation resulting from the ongoing conversion reaction during repeating cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54,55)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Owing to the superior conductivity of the N-doped graphene nanosheets, the FS–NGNs display excellent rate performance, as can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, where the current density steps up from 0.1 to 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The FS–NGNs maintain capacities of 705, 712, 666, 650, 637, 581, 567, and 543 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 1.0, 3.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, the discharge capacity returns to 762 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> as the 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> current density is recovered. And the corresponding charge–discharge voltage profiles shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> also maintain good conformity, exhibiting good robustness of the electrode. In conclusion, the excellent electrochemical performance of FS–NGNs is a remarkable result among iron sulfide-based anodes in the recent reports for SIBs, especially the high capacity after long-term cycling and high-rate stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis was conducted for more detailed understanding of the role of N-doped graphene nanosheets in the enhancement of the conductivity of the FS–NGNs electrode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9, and Table S2</a>, the semicircle at the intermediate-frequency area can be identified as the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). Before cycling, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the FS–NGNs is 50.9 Ω, which exhibits much lower resistance than that of bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (140 Ω) before the reaction, mainly benefiting from the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanocrystals and the high conductivity of N-doped graphene nanosheets. After 200 cycles, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the FS–NGNs increases to 109.8 Ω, while the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> rises up significantly (273.6 Ω) due to the destruction of the structure during ongoing Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and extraction. Thus, the EIS result is evident to prove the high conductivity of the FS–NGNs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To better understand the influence of the nanostructure on the cycling stability of FS–NGNs, the morphology of the FS–NGNs after 500 cycles at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup> is investigated by SEM and TEM. As can be seen, the overall morphology of the N-doped graphene nanosheets remained without restacking, while the initial Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanocrystals are further converted into smaller particles anchored uniformly on the surface of the NGNs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10</a>). The above result are in good accordance with previous analysis of capacity increase. The maintained nanosheet structure can be further confirmed by the TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), where the uniform distribution of the cycled product can be observed clearly. The HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c) exhibits the lattice <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.25 nm, which is corresponding to the (101) plane of FeS. The SAED pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) also demonstrates series of diffraction rings correlated to (311), (220), and (101) planes of FeS, together with (102) and (100) planes of metal Fe, which is beneficial for improving the conductivity of the electrode. It is interesting to find out that the cycled products are FeS instead of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. Actually, we have also analyzed the electrode after 100 cycles. The SAED pattern and HRTEM image exhibited that Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> still existed, while no FeS was detected. This indicates that at least after 100 cycles the reversibility of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is satisfying (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/suppl_file/am8b08237_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). However, as discussed previously, after repeating sodiation/desodiation for hundreds of times, the ongoing new interface exposed among particles and continuous SEI formation may cause an active component surrounded by SEI to form “isolated islands”. Moreover, the Fe nanoparticles formed during the discharge process tend to aggregate, whereas the intermediate sodium polysulfide can react with the carbonate ester electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> The above factors may reduce the amounts of active materials and change the distribution and the Fe/S ratio of the cycled products in the nanoscale. On the other hand, the ion/electron transport in the “isolated islands” is blocked, whereas the intrinsic sluggish kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> transport makes it even harder for metallic Fe to be oxidized into Fe<sup>2+</sup> than Fe<sup>3+</sup>, which means that the full conversion has encountered more obstacles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Thus, the above discussion can explain the change in the conversion mechanism after 500 cycles. Nonetheless, this may not be an evidence to claim that FeS possesses better sodium-ion storage performance than Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. Firstly, the unthorough conversion reaction also exists for FeS, because the conversion efficiency cannot reach 100%. Secondly, the theoretical capacity of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is higher than FeS owing to its smaller Fe/S ratio. Thirdly, the mixed-valence state of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> endows it with higher electronic conductivity.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/medium/am-2018-08237t_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) SEM, (b) TEM, (c) HRTEM, and (d) SAED images of the cycled FS–NGNs. High-resolution XPS analysis of the cycled FS–NGNs: (e) Fe 2p and (f) S 2p. (g) Illustration of the cycling process of the FS–NGNs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08237/asset/images/large/am-2018-08237t_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08237&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further analyze the composition of the cycled product and understand the reaction during cycles, we carried out the XPS study on the cycled FS–NSN electrode. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e, the fitted peaks at 712.1 and 716 eV are corresponding to Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, whereas the peaks at 720, 724.3, and 730 eV are correlated with Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>. Among them, the peaks at 720 eV is the indication of metallic Fe, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> whereas the other four peaks are in accordance with Fe<sup>2+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 58 to 61">(58−61)</a> As for the S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f), the peak at 162.8 eV indicates the existence of metal sulfide, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> whereas the satellite peak at 169 eV could be ascribed to some surface absorbed oxidized S species, such as SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> and HSO<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> This composition characterization further confirms the HRTEM and SAED analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Thus, the FS–NGNs can maintain good structural stability and high conductivity, ensuring stable cyclability and excellent rate performance. The corresponding cycling process is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To sum up, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles anchored on N-doped graphene nanosheets (FS–NGNs) fabricated by a simple solvothermal reaction combined with postheating treatment have been proved as a suitable anode material with high capacity and high-rate cyclability for SIBs. The solid attachment of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and its cycling product to the N-doped graphene nanosheets is crucial for building up a robust structure to endure the repeated Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process. The FS–NGNs retain 391.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles at 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity can maintain 543 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. In general, this study has presented a feasible strategy to develop high-performance metal sulfide-based hybrid anodes of future SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 nanosheets decorated Ni3S2@MoS2 coaxial nanofibers: Constructing an ideal heterostructure for enhanced Na-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jin; Liu, Jilei; Yang, Hao; Chao, Dongliang; Yan, Jiaxu; Savilov, Serguei V.; Lin, Jianyi; Shen, Ze Xiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1-10</span>CODEN:
