                        cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fe/Fe3C@C nanoparticles encapsulated in N-doped graphene-CNTs framework as an efficient bifunctional oxygen electrocatalyst for robust rechargeable Zn-air batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Qichen; Lei, Yongpeng; Chen, Zhiyan; Wu, Nan; Wang, Yaobing; Wang, Bing; Wang, Yingde</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">516-526</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">3D transition metals or their derivs. encapsulated in nitrogen-doped nanocarbon show promising potential in non-precious metal oxygen electrocatalysts.  Herein, we describe the simple construction of a bifunctional oxygen electrocatalyst with a "framework-active sites" structure, namely Fe/Fe3C@C (Fe@C) nanoparticles encapsulated in 3D N-doped graphene and bamboo-like CNTs (Fe@C-NG/NCNTs).  The Fe@C structure provides addnl. electrons on the carbon surface, promoting the oxygen redn. reaction (ORR) on adjacent Fe-Nx active sites.  The 3D NG hybrid with a bamboo-like CNTs framework facilitates fast reactant diffusion and rapid electron transfer.  The optimized sample displays excellent ORR and oxygen evolution reaction (OER) activity, with a p.d. of only 0.84 V; this places it among the best bifunctional ORR/OER electrocatalysts.  Most importantly, Zn-air batteries using Fe@C-NG/NCNTs as the cathode catalyst deliver a peak power d. of 101.2 mW cm-2 and a specific capacity of 682.6 mA h g-1 (energy d. of 764.5 W h kg-1).  After 297 continuous cycle tests (99 h), the rechargeable batteries using Fe@C-NG/NCNTs show a voltage gap increase of only 0.13 V, almost half that of Pt/C + Ir/C (0.22 V) under the same conditions.  This work provides new insight into advanced electrocatalysts utilizing the structural features of host nanocarbon materials and guest active species toward energy conversion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrX6nVo2RhRabVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHG4XIqfDH9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVSktbzP&amp;md5=a8bfdfbd5878db307d2cfe6e1129448a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit59" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201706640"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhai, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Achieving Insertion-Like Capacity at Ultrahigh Rate via Tunable Surface Pseudocapacitance</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hierarchical FeCo2O4@NiCo layered double hydroxide core/shell nanowires for high performance flexible all-solid-state asymmetric supercapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Xinyi; Li, Rumin; Liu, Jingyuan; Liu, Qi; chen, RongRong; Song, Dalei; Wang, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">334</span>

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible supercapacitors are important energy storage devices used for wearable and smart electronics.  The key to fabricating flexible supercapacitors lies in the acquirement of flexible electrodes.  However, design and fabrication of flexible electrodes with both high energy and power densities as well as long cycling life is still a challenge work.  Herein, a novel flexible supercapacitor electrode composed of hierarchical FeCo2O4@NiCo-LDH core/shell heterostructures on carbon cloth is reported by a facile and cost-effective method.  The electrode exhibits a remarkable specific capacitance of 2426 Fg-1 at 1Ag-1, and ultrahigh rate capability with 72.5% capacitance retention at 20Ag-1, as well as excellent cycling stability with a capacitance retention of 91.6% after 5000 cycles.  Furthermore, the FeCo2O4@NiCo-LDH/CC as the pos. electrode, active carbon as the neg. electrode, and PVA-KOH gel as both the solid state electrolyte and separator were assembled into flexible all-solid-state asym. supercapacitor.  The resulting FeCo2O4@NiCo-LDH//AC ASC device exhibits a max. energy d. of 94.9 Whkg-1, and a favorable energy d. of 66.5Whkg-1 is still achieved at a high power d. of 1.6 kWkg-1, as well as excellent cycling stability of 88.2% after 5000 cycles.  Moreover, the ASC device exhibits outstanding flexibility and reliability with no degrdn. under large twisting, which can light one red LED indicators efficiently.  The work presented here hold promise in energy storage for future portable and wearable electronic devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGopssNquzi2j7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHG4XIqfDH9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVKltrbJ&amp;md5=e2221e374b4190b30f8c6f52288ac8a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit61" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.97.054509"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hazi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mousavi, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dudin, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van der Laan, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maccherozzi, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Krzton-Maziopa, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pomjakushina, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Conder, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Speller, S.             This article references
            63 other publications.
        </p><ol id="rightTab-references" class="useLabel"><li id="rightTab-ref1"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref1" class="rightTabRefNumLink">1</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit1" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.12.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Savilov, S. V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Z. X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">MoS<sub>2</sub> Nanosheets Decorated Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> Coaxial Nanofibers: Constructing an Ideal Heterostructure for Enhanced Na-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>20</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">10</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.12.010</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 nanosheets decorated Ni3S2@MoS2 coaxial nanofibers: Constructing an ideal heterostructure for enhanced Na-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jin; Liu, Jilei; Yang, Hao; Chao, Dongliang; Yan, Jiaxu; Savilov, Serguei V.; Lin, Jianyi; Shen, Ze Xiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1-10</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Abstr.: The performance of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) is mainly detd. by the electrochem. activity and kinetic feature of electrode materials.  High performance relies largely on the scrupulous design of nano-architectures and smart hybridization of bespoke active materials.  It is fundamentally important for establishing a relationship between the structure/chem. of these materials and their properties.  Herein, we developed a novel synergistic Ni3S2-MoS2 core-shell nanofiber superstructure on 3D Ni/graphene foam by a one-step PVP-assisted hydrothermal reaction.  Such hierarchical nanofibers can provide the homogeneous at. heterointerface with porous hierarchical structure, resulting in the maximization of synergistic interaction.  This unique structure results in very high specific capacity and rate capability as well as extremely long-term cycle stability.  As anode electrode of SIBs, it exhibits a very high reversible specific capacity of 568 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1 with excellent rate capability (283 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1), and the specific capacity can be well-maintained to 207 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 even after 400 cycles.  The strategy developed in our study can open a new way to prep. other layered-material-based hybrid superstructure for next-generation energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0yN_ht7qvabVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljXwhr4Gbv5Cg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XptVCh&amp;md5=ea264d469df97543894615c2d07b72d6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c2nr31822a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, L.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile Synthesis of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CMK-3 Nanocomposite as an Improved Anode Material for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nanoscale</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5868</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5871</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as complementary alternatives to lithium-ion batteries for grid energy storage due to the abundance of sodium.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are an alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale applications.  However, low capacity and poor rate capability of existing anodes are the main bottlenecks to future developments.  Here we report a uniform coating of antimony sulphide (stibnite) on graphene, fabricated by a solution-based synthesis technique, as the anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  It gives a high capacity of 730 mAh g(-1) at 50 mA g(-1), an excellent rate capability up to 6C and a good cycle performance.  The promising performance is attributed to fast sodium ion diffusion from the small nanoparticles, and good electrical transport from the intimate contact between the active material and graphene, which also provides a template for anchoring the nanoparticles.  We also demonstrate a battery with the stibnite-graphene composite that is free from sodium metal, having energy density up to 80 Wh kg(-1).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Exploitation of high-performance anode materials is essential but challenging to the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). A facile solvothermal method to in situ decorate cobalt sulfide (CoS) nanoplates on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) to build CoS@rGO composite is described.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the synthesis and electrochem. sodium storage of cobalt disulfide (CoS2) with various micro/nano-structures.  CoS2 with microscale sizes are either assembled by nanoparticles (P-CoS2) via a facile solvothermal route or nanooctahedrons constructed solid (O-CoS2) and hollow microstructures (H-CoS2) fabricated by hydrothermal methods.  Among three morphologies, H-CoS2 exhibits the largest discharge capacities and best rate performance as anode of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Furthermore, H-CoS2 delivers a capacity of 690 mA·h·g-1 at 1 A·g-1 after 100 cycles in a potential range of 0.1-3.0 V, and ∼240 mA·h·g-1 over 800 cycles in the potential window of 1.0-3.0 V.  This cycling difference mainly lies in the two discharge plateaus obsd. in 0.1-3.0 V and one discharge plateau in 1.0-3.0 V.  To interpret the reactions, X-ray diffraction (XRD) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) are applied.  The results show that at the first plateau around 1.4 V, the insertion reaction (CoS2 + xNa+ + xe- → NaxCoS2) occurs; while at the second plateau around 0.6 V, the conversion reaction (NaxCoS2 + (4 - x) Na+ + (4 - x)e- → Co + 2Na2S) takes place.  This provides insights for electrochem. sodium storage of CoS2 as the anode of SIBs.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-metal sulfide battery holds great promise for sustainable and cost-effective applications.  Nevertheless, achieving high capacity and cycling stability remains a great challenge.  Here, uniform yolk-shell iron sulfide-carbon nanospheres have been synthesized as cathode materials for the emerging sodium sulfide battery to achieve remarkable capacity of ∼545 mA h g-1 over 100 cycles at 0.2 C (100 mA g-1), delivering ultrahigh energy d. of ∼438 Wh kg-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is desirable to develop electrode materials for advanced rechargeable batteries with low cost, long life, and high-rate capability.  Pyrite FeS2, as an easily obtained natural mineral, has been already commercialized in primary lithium batteries, but encountered problems in rechargeable batteries with carbonate-based electrolytes due to the limited cycle life caused by the conversion-type reaction (FeS2 + 4M → Fe + 2M2S (M = Li or Na)).  Herein, we demonstrate that FeS2 microspheres can be applied in room-temp. rechargeable sodium batteries with only the intercalation reaction by simultaneously selecting a compatible NaSO3CF3/diglyme electrolyte and tuning the cut-off voltage to 0.8 V. To be one of the most active O evolution reaction catalysts in alk. media, Ni-Fe based compds. have attracted tremendous attention, while the role of Ni and Fe sites played has still come under debate.  Herein, by taking the pyrrhotite Fe7S8 nanosheets with mixed-valence states and metallic cond. for examples, the authors illustrate that Fe could be a highly active site for electrocatalytic O evolution.  The delocalized electrons in the ultrathin Fe7S8 nanosheets could facilitate electron transfer processes of the system, where d orbitals of FeII and FeIII would be overlapped with each other during the catalytic reactions, rendering the ultrathin Fe7S8 nanosheets to be the most efficient Fe-based electrocatalyst for H2O oxidn.  As expected, the ultrathin Fe7S8 nanosheets exhibit promising electrocatalytic O evolution activities, with a low overpotential of 0.27 V and a large c.d. of 300 mA cm-2 at 0.5 V.  This work provides solid evidence that Fe could be an efficient active site for electrocatalytic H2O splitting.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzVBbUpzMP67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljLbM3syUqqVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhslSht7%252FO&amp;md5=02bdc60a0964d05bf89c5e18f5b81776</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1021/acssuschemeng.7b00430"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ci, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Si, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chi, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Metal-Organic Framework Derived Iron Sulfide-Carbon Core-Shell Nanorods as a Conversion-Type Battery Material</span>. <i>ACS Sustainable Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljLbM3syUqqVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06708H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-Pot Synthesis of Ultra-Small Magnetite Nanoparticles on the Surface of Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanosheets as Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Supercapacitors, also called ultracapacitors or electrochem. capacitors, store elec. charge on high-surface-area conducting materials.                         rightTab-cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Doping carbons beyond nitrogen: an overview of advanced heteroatom doped carbons with boron, sulphur and phosphorus for energy applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Paraknowitsch, Jens Peter; Thomas, Arne</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2839-2855</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Heteroatom doped carbon materials represent one of the most prominent families of materials that are used in energy related applications, such as fuel cells, batteries, hydrogen storage or supercapacitors.  While doping carbons with nitrogen atoms has experienced great progress throughout the past decades and yielded promising material concepts, also other doping candidates have gained the researchers' interest in the last few years.  Boron is already relatively widely studied, and as its electronic situation is contrary to the one of nitrogen, codoping carbons with both heteroatoms can probably create synergistic effects.  Sulfur and phosphorus have just recently entered the world of carbon synthesis, but already the first studies published prove their potential, esp. as electrocatalysts in the cathodic compartment of fuel cells.  Due to their size and their electronegativity being lower than those of carbon, structural distortions and changes of the charge densities are induced in the carbon materials.  This article is to give a state of the art update on the most recent developments concerning the advanced heteroatom doping of carbon that goes beyond nitrogen.  Doped carbon materials and their applications in energy devices are discussed with respect to their boron-, sulfur- and phosphorus-doping.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrXYE3FbkNibrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljLbM3syUqqVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsV2it7fO&amp;md5=182291f9157be822fc6701fac47f4c00</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1021/ja01539a017"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hummers, W.                         rightTab-cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Langmuir-Blodgett assembly of graphite oxide single layers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cote, Laura J.; Kim, Franklin; Huang, Jiaxing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Single-layer graphite oxide can be viewed as an unconventional type of soft material and has recently been recognized as a promising material for composite and electronics applications.  It is of both scientific curiosity and tech. importance to know how these atomically thin sheets assemble.  There are two fundamental geometries of interacting single layers: edge-to-edge and face-to-face.  Such interactions were studied at the air-water interface by Langmuir-Blodgett assembly.  Stable monolayers of graphite oxide single layers were obtained without the need for any surfactant or stabilizing agent, due to the strong electrostatic repulsion between the 2-dimensional confined layers.  Such repulsion also prevented the single layers from overlapping during compression, leading to excellent reversibility of the monolayers.  In contrast to mol. and hard colloidal particle monolayers, the single layers tend to fold and wrinkle at edges to resist collapsing into multilayers.  The monolayers can be transferred to a substrate, readily creating a large area of flat graphite oxide single layers.  The d. of such films can be continuously tuned from dil., close-packed to overpacked monolayers of interlocking single layers.  For size-mismatched single layers, face-to-face interaction caused irreversible stacking, leading to double layers.  The graphite oxide monolayers can be chem. reduced to graphene for electronic applications such as transparent conducting thin films.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpt2ZLgdRwpVLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljbOxHPmta_Pw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXht1Olsb%252FK&amp;md5=aa7702e74e7abde5a9b02a719a9042b2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2017.07.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Truong, T.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Clin. applications of current photodynamic therapy (PDT) agents are often restricted to be activated only by UV and visible light, which have very poor tissue penetration depths.  In this study, a new near IR (NIR)-absorbing nanoagent based on graphene oxide decorated with iron hydroxide/oxide (GO-FeOxH) was developed for light-activated nanomaterial-mediated PDT.  This nanocomposite, GO-FeOxH was prepd. via the one-step electrooxidn. of iron nails in an aq. GO soln.  The as-prepd.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we have investigated the band structures of ternary Cu4Sn7+xS16 (x = 0-1.0) compds. with an excess of Sn, and examd. their thermoelec. (TE) properties.  First principles calcns. reveal that the excess Sn, which exists as Sn2+ and is preferentially located at the intrinsic Cu vacancies, unpins the Fermi level (Fr) and allows Fr to enter the conduction band (CB) at x = 0.5.  Accordingly, the Hall carrier concn. (nH) is enhanced by about two orders of magnitude when the x value increases from x = 0 to x = 0.5.  Meanwhile, the lattice thermal cond. (κL) is reduced significantly to 0.39 W K-1 m-1 at 893 K, which is in reasonably good agreement with the estn. using the Callaway model.                         rightTab-cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NiCo2S4 nanocrystals anchored on nitrogen-doped carbon nanotubes as a highly efficient bifunctional electrocatalyst for rechargeable zinc-air batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Xiaopeng; Wu, Xiaoyu; Zhong, Cheng; Deng, Yida; Zhao, Naiqin; Hu, Wenbin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The com. development of metal-air batteries with remarkably high theor. energy output is largely limited by the scarcity of low-cost and highly stable electrocatalysts with activities for oxygen redn. reaction (ORR) and oxygen evolution reaction (OER) comparable to precious metals and their alloys.  Herein, a new inorg.-nanocarbon coupled hybrid, homogeneous NiCo2S4 nanocrystals anchored on nitrogen-doped carbon nanotubes (NiCo2S4/N-CNT), has been developed as an extremely efficient bifunctional catalyst to promote the sluggish ORR and OER kinetics for advanced rechargeable zinc-air battery.  It is found that the highest activity is obtained by tailoring the crystal size of NiCo2S4 in the hybrid through tuning the construction of metal-ammonia complexes.  The optimized NiCo2S4/N-CNT nanocomposite exhibits extraordinary bifunctional activity through half reaction testing, displaying comparable ORR activity to state-of-the-art carbon supported platinum (Pt/C) and superior OER capability to RuO2 as well as much better durability.  The resulting battery performance in generative Zn-air system further confirms its superb effective bi-functionality for catalyzing dual ORR and OER.  Compared with those of well-known com. Pt/C and RuO2, the synergetic NiCo2S4/N-CNT hybrid enables significantly reduced charge-discharge polarization (∼0.63 V), enlarged energy efficiency (∼67.2%) and prolonged cyclability up to 150 cycles at 10 mA cm-2.  The tremendously enhanced electrochem. behaviors arise from favorable factors including small sized, homogenous dispersed novel NiCo2S4 nanocrystals and coupling interaction between sulfide spinels and underlying N-doped CNT network, which not only provides efficient electron transfer pathway but also alters the electronic structure, thus facilitating the oxygen electrocatalysis during the discharge-charge processes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5L_uxVO5lFLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgtc_sla3HVKw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitV2mur%252FI&amp;md5=f474119eeec64b5c20719ae25453d881</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701527"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mei, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">3D Printing Sulfur Copolymer-Graphene Architectures for Li-S Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-org. framework (MOF)-derived hollow cobalt sulfides have attracted extensive attention due to their porous shell that provides rich redox reactions for energy storage.  However, their ultradispersed structure and the large size of MOF precursors result in relatively low cond., stability, and tap d.  Therefore, the construction of an array of continuous hollow cages and tailoring of the inner cavity of MOF-derived materials is very effective for enhancing the electrochem. performance.  Herein, we in situ assembled small Co-based zeolitic imidazolate framework (ZIF-67) on both sides of neg. charged MnO2 nanosheets to fabricate a hierarchical sandwich-type composite with hollow cobalt sulfide nanocages/graphene-like MnO2.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As a potential alternative to the prevailing lithium ion batteries, the application of sodium (Na) ion batteries (NIBs) in renewable energy and smart grid have revitalized research interest for large-scale energy storage.  One of the roadblocks hindering their future commercialization is the development of suitable anode materials.  Herein, we present the large-scale prepn. of highly uniform iron sulfide (Fe1-xS) nanostructures by a cost-effective and versatile one-step sulfurization strategy.  Impressively, as a high-rate and viable sodium-ion anode, the as-prepd. Fe1-xS nanostructure manifests appealing electrochem. performance (a high discharge capacity of 563 mA h g-1 over 200 cycles at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 and outstanding cycling stability even at high rate of 10 A g-1 up to 2000 cycles). Meanwhile, the sodium storage mechanism in the as-prepd. samples has also been investigated by using the in-situ X-ray diffraction techniques.                         rightTab-cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A graphene-like MoS2/graphene nanocomposite as a high performance anode for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Zhao, Yanping; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">32</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report on the prepn. of a graphene-like MoS2/graphene nanocomposite by hydrolysis of lithiated MoS2 (LiMoS2) and its application as the anode material for Li ion batteries.  When the mass ratio of graphene/LiMoS2 is 15/100, the obtained composite (MoS2/GNS-15) displays a flower-like architecture composed of exfoliated nanosheets.  The structure analyses further demonstrate that graphene-like MoS2 is supported on the surface of graphene nanosheets (GNS) and some of the interlayer spacings of MoS2 are enlarged with the intercalation of graphene.  The reversible capacity of the MoS2/GNS-15 nanocomposite is ∼1400 mA h g-1 in the initial cycle and remains 1351 mA h g-1 after 200 cycles at 100 mA g-1.  Also, the capacity can reach 591 mA h g-1 even at a high c.d. of 1000 mA g-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance of MoS2/GNS-15 is due to the synergetic effect between highly conductive GNS and graphene-like MoS2.  On one hand, the GNS matrix can offer 2-dimensional conductive networks and effectively suppress the aggregation of layered MoS2 during the lithiation/delithiation process.  However, graphene-like MoS2 with an enlarged gallery can ensure the flooding of the electrolyte, provide more active sites and lower the diffusion energy barrier of Li+ ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpMiK4ZbJ2OTrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhKG0NDtsjaGQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtFeiu7bK&amp;md5=5cf7020869ef3e1a0350e91a61863e59</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1039/C4NR05106H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exfoliated-SnS<sub>2</sub> Restacked on Graphene as a High-Capacity, High-Rate, and Long-Cycle Life Anode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nanoscale</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1325</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1332</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Designed as a high-capacity, high-rate, and long-cycle life anode for sodium ion batteries, exfoliated-SnS2 restacked on graphene is prepd. by the hydrolysis of lithiated SnS2 followed by a facile hydrothermal method.  Structural and morphol. characterizations demonstrate that ultrasmall SnS2 nanoplates (with a typical size of 20-50 nm) composed of 2-5 layers are homogeneously decorated on the surface of graphene, while the hybrid structure self-assembles into a three-dimensional (3D) network architecture.  The obtained SnS2/graphene nanocomposite delivers a remarkable capacity as high as 650 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  More impressively, the capacity can reach 326 mA h g-1 even at 4000 mA g-1 and remains stable at ∼610 mA h g-1 without fading up to 300 cycles when the rate is brought back to 200 mA g-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance is attributed to the synergetic effects between the ultrasmall SnS2 and the highly conductive graphene network.  The unique structure can simultaneously facilitate Na+ ion diffusion, provide more reaction sites, and suppress aggregation and vol. fluctuation of the active materials during prolonged cycling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRwuFHeQ2-ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhKG0NDtsjaGQ"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A promising anode material consisting of Fe1-xS nanoparticles and bamboo-like carbon nanotubes (CNTs) has been designed and prepd. by an effective in situ chem. transformation.  The resultant Fe1-xS@CNTs with a three-dimensional network not only provide high cond. paths and channels for electrons and ions but also offer the combined merits of iron sulfide and CNTs in electrochem. energy storage applications, leading to outstanding performance as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries due to the natural abundance of sodium resources.  Searching for high-performance anode materials currently becomes a hot topic and also a great challenge for developing Na-ion batteries.  In this work, a novel hybrid anode is synthesized consisting of ultrafine, few-layered SnS2 anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a facile solvothermal route.  The SnS2/rGO hybrid exhibits a high capacity, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate capability.  The hybrid can deliver a high charge capacity of 649 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g. The hybrid can also sustain a c.d. up to 12.8 A/g (≈28C) where the charge process can be completed in only 1.3 min while still delivering a charge capacity of 337 mA-h/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel anode material for sodium-ion batteries consisting of 3D graphene microspheres divided into several tens of uniform nanospheres coated with few-layered MoS2 by a one-pot spray pyrolysis process is prepd.  The first discharge/charge capacities of the composite microspheres are 797 and 573 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 0.2 A g-1.  The 600th discharge capacity of the composite microspheres at a c.d. of 1.5 A g-1 is 322 mA h g-1.  The Coulombic efficiency during the 600 cycles is as high as 99.98%.  The outstanding Na ion storage properties of the 3D MoS2-graphene composite microspheres may be attributed to the reduced stacking of the MoS2 layers and to the 3D structure of the porous graphene microspheres.  The reduced stacking of the MoS2 layers relaxes the strain and lowers the barrier for Na+ insertion.                         rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unusual Formation of ZnCo2O4 3D Hierarchical Twin Microspheres as a High-Rate and Ultralong-Life Lithium-Ion Battery Anode Material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bai, Jing; Li, Xiaogang; Liu, Guangzeng; Qian, Yitai; Xiong, Shenglin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">24</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A facile two-step strategy involving a polyol method and subsequent thermal annealing treatment is successfully developed for the large-scale prepn. of ZnCo2O4 various hierarchical micro/nanostructures (twin microspheres and microcubes) without surfactant assistance.  To the best of our knowledge, this is the first report on the synthesis of ZnCo2O4 mesoporous twin microspheres and microcubes.  More significantly, based on the effect of the reaction time on the morphol. evolution of the precursor, a brand-new crystal growth mechanism, multistep splitting then in situ dissoln. recrystn. accompanied by morphol. and phase change, is first proposed to understand the formation of the three-dimensional (3D) twin microspheres, providing new research opportunity for investigating the formation of novel micro/nanostructures.  When evaluated as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries, ZnCo2O4 hierarchical microstructures exhibit superior capacity retention, excellent cycling stability at the 5 A/g rate for 2000 cycles.  Surprisingly, the ZnCo2O4 twin microspheres show an exceptionally high rate capability up to the 10 A/g rate.  It should be noted that such super-high rate performance and cycling stability at such high charge/discharge rates are significantly higher than most work previously reported on ZnCo2O4 micro/nanostructures and ZnCo2O4-based heterostructures.  The ZnCo2O4 3D hierarchical micro/nanostructures demonstrate the great potential as anode materials for high-performance lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdA9oVZgVx67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljeyHv89rJK9g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXivVCisLs%253D&amp;md5=5f7f2085cbca2d3a1ff305a3caad868d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201300755"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rui, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sim, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hoster, H.                         rightTab-cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Role of transition metal nanoparticles in the extra lithium storage capacity of transition metal oxides: a case study of hierarchical core-shell Fe3O4@C and Fe@C microspheres</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Liwei; Zhong, Yiren; Zhou, Zhen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The conversion reaction mechanism has widely been accepted in interpreting and evaluating the lithium storage capability of transition metal oxides (MOs).  However, this mechanism cannot well explain the phenomenon of the extra capacity which exists in almost all MO materials and attracts much attention.  Up to now, the extra capacity phenomenon has generally been ascribed to the reversible conversion of polymeric gel-like films.  However, the essential role of metal nanoparticles in this process has not been systematically investigated.  To further illustrate the role of metal nanoparticles for the extra capacity, Fe3O4@C and Fe@C monodispersed hierarchical core-shell microspheres were designed and adopted as the case study.  Naturally Fe3O4@C composites exhibited a large Li storage capacity beyond its theor. value.  However, Fe@C microspheres, which are usually regarded to be inert for lithium storage, still presented a certain electrochem. capacity.  Fe nanoparticles might serve as electrocatalysts for the reversible conversion of some components of solid electrolyte interface films, and bring extra capacity to Fe3O4 and electrochem. capacity to Fe.  This study can enlighten us for the exploiting of advanced active materials and electrolytes for Li ion batteries, and new energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqjQmjp2TIgbrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liZCjYpduOyDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslOktrvJ&amp;md5=b53a4841d30d0fc7d08c9a7406af0c74</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201703671"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.                         rightTab-cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The dramatic enhancement of ferromagnetism and band gap in Fe-doped In2O3 nanodot arrays</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang Feng-Xian; Chen Dan; Zhou Guo-Wei; Wang Ya-Nan; Xu Xiao-Hong; Jiang Feng-Xian; Chen Dan; Zhou Guo-Wei; Wang Ya-Nan; Xu Xiao-Hong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific reports</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Ordered Fe-doped In2O3 nanodot arrays with diameters between 35 nm and 80 nm are fabricated using pulsed laser deposition with the aid of ultrathin porous anodized aluminumoxide templates.  The 5 at.% Fe doped In2O3 nanodot arrays are shown to consist of the cubic bixbyite structure of In2O3.  The nanodot arrays are demonstrated to be doped by Fe ions with mixed valences of +2 and +3, ruling out the presence of cluster and secondary phase related to Fe.  The nanodot arrays exhibit the ferromagnetism at room temperature, where the magnetic moment increases as the dot size is reduced, rising to a maximum of about 230 emu/cm(3) (equivalent to an average moment on the Fe ions of 15.30 μB/Fe).  This indicates an effect due to the surface of the nanodot arrays.  The optical band width is also increased to 4.55 eV for the smallest dot array, thus indicating that the surface states are responsible for the magnetism and also enhance the band gap due to Burstein-Moss effect.         </div><div class="casAbstract">3D transition metals or their derivs. encapsulated in nitrogen-doped nanocarbon show promising potential in non-precious metal oxygen electrocatalysts.  Herein, we describe the simple construction of a bifunctional oxygen electrocatalyst with a "framework-active sites" structure, namely Fe/Fe3C@C (Fe@C) nanoparticles encapsulated in 3D N-doped graphene and bamboo-like CNTs (Fe@C-NG/NCNTs). The 3D NG hybrid with a bamboo-like CNTs framework facilitates fast reactant diffusion and rapid electron transfer.  The optimized sample displays excellent ORR and oxygen evolution reaction (OER) activity, with a p.d. of only 0.84 V; this places it among the best bifunctional ORR/OER electrocatalysts.  Most importantly, Zn-air batteries using Fe@C-NG/NCNTs as the cathode catalyst deliver a peak power d. of 101.2 mW cm-2 and a specific capacity of 682.6 mA h g-1 (energy d. of 764.5 W h kg-1).  After 297 continuous cycle tests (99 h), the rechargeable batteries using Fe@C-NG/NCNTs show a voltage gap increase of only 0.13 V, almost half that of Pt/C + Ir/C (0.22 V) under the same conditions.  This work provides new insight into advanced electrocatalysts utilizing the structural features of host nanocarbon materials and guest active species toward energy conversion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrX6nVo2RhRabVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liZCjYpduOyDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVSktbzP&amp;md5=a8bfdfbd5878db307d2cfe6e1129448a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref59" class="rightTabRefNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit59" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201706640"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhai, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Achieving Insertion-Like Capacity at Ultrahigh Rate via Tunable Surface Pseudocapacitance</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1706640</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201706640</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2017.11.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">chen, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical FeCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@NiCo Layered Double Hydroxide Core/Shell Nanowires for High Performance Flexible All-Solid-State Asymmetric Supercapacitors</span>. <i>Chem. Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>334</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1573</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1583</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2017.11.089</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hierarchical FeCo2O4@NiCo layered double hydroxide core/shell nanowires for high performance flexible all-solid-state asymmetric supercapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Xinyi; Li, Rumin; Liu, Jingyuan; Liu, Qi; chen, RongRong; Song, Dalei; Wang, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">334</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1573-1583</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible supercapacitors are important energy storage devices used for wearable and smart electronics.  The key to fabricating flexible supercapacitors lies in the acquirement of flexible electrodes.  However, design and fabrication of flexible electrodes with both high energy and power densities as well as long cycling life is still a challenge work.  Herein, a novel flexible supercapacitor electrode composed of hierarchical FeCo2O4@NiCo-LDH core/shell heterostructures on carbon cloth is reported by a facile and cost-effective method.  The electrode exhibits a remarkable specific capacitance of 2426 Fg-1 at 1Ag-1, and ultrahigh rate capability with 72.5% capacitance retention at 20Ag-1, as well as excellent cycling stability with a capacitance retention of 91.6% after 5000 cycles.  Furthermore, the FeCo2O4@NiCo-LDH/CC as the pos. electrode, active carbon as the neg. electrode, and PVA-KOH gel as both the solid state electrolyte and separator were assembled into flexible all-solid-state asym. supercapacitor.  The resulting FeCo2O4@NiCo-LDH//AC ASC device exhibits a max. energy d. of 94.9 Whkg-1, and a favorable energy d. of 66.5Whkg-1 is still achieved at a high power d. of 1.6 kWkg-1, as well as excellent cycling stability of 88.2% after 5000 cycles.  Moreover, the ASC device exhibits outstanding flexibility and reliability with no degrdn. under large twisting, which can light one red LED indicators efficiently.  The work presented here hold promise in energy storage for future portable and wearable electronic devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGopssNquzi2j7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liZCjYpduOyDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVKltrbJ&amp;md5=e2221e374b4190b30f8c6f52288ac8a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.97.054509"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hazi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mousavi, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dudin, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van der Laan, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maccherozzi, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Krzton-Maziopa, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pomjakushina, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Conder, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Speller, S. C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Magnetic Imaging of Antiferromagnetic and Superconducting Phases in Rb<sub>x</sub>Fe<sub>2–y</sub>Se<sub>2</sub> Crystals</span>. <i>Phys.                 10.1103/PhysRevB.97.054509</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();

	});

	});

<script nonce="90da8fb75e5db325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

)|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
